0307454541|4.0|Because one of the two loosely interlinked stories in \\"The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo\\" is far more compelling than the other, the end of the book proves disappointing, since it was necessary for the more interesting story to be concluded first - a good 100 pages from the end of the book. Thus, keeping reader interest to the last page proves impossible for Steig Larsson, though he did his best by continuing to involve most of the key players in narrating the end to the less interesting second story. That is the main problem in this book: the thin almost non-existent connection between the two primary story lines, and the necessity of ending the book with the less interesting one.<br /><br />What link the two stories together are the main players, Blomkvist and Salander and some of their side-kicks and compatriots. Their actual link-up did not occur, however, for at least the first 300 pages as their separate life stories were developed without overlap. Thus, it was a very long lead-up to their collaboration.  Too long?  Yes. Way too long.<br /><br />Thus, we have the other great flaw in this novel: it is too long (590 pages). While the stories are often very good and engrossing (except for the incomprehensible Swedish geography detail), the book itself is, in my estimation, at least 100+ pages too long. The extra length was sheer fluff, existing on virtually every page and often several pages long - a clear case of \\"too much information\\" (TMI).<br /><br />At times there is a very good, exciting pace to the tale, especially, as an example, when the evil one decides to try to murder Blomkvist with a rifle.<br /><br />Quite a bit of the central story is grisly, almost hard core and brutal pornography - gruesomely difficult to read at times. But after all, the central message of the book revolves around abuse and murder of innocent women. Even the telling about past abuse done to others is awful, let alone the experiencing of it by Salander and -- one time only -- by Blomkvist. Incest, murder and torture become major themes in the book.<br /><br />\\"The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo\\" is quite well written and for the most part interesting (except for the secondary story - a world-wide economic gangster scam -- which is, frankly rather dull). The translation, performed by 65 year-old Reg Keeland (who lives in Albuquerque, New Mexico), is admirable, though the pages are often peppered with long unused American slang phrases from the 1950s and 1960s, a function of his generation, no doubt and how he perhaps mistakenly believes Swedes talk casually to each other or write. All-in-all, though, it's a zippy translation and quite contemporary. He does an especially good job translating 24 year-old Salander's voice.<br /><br />Salander is by far the most interesting character in the book, completely and fully consistent from one end of the book to the other. She is maddening and funny, sexy and unappealing, smart and dumb all at the same time, but always (almost always anyway) fully on top of everything that occurs in her young life. She is a women's advocate, a champion for abused women everywhere, and with good reason. Her own life is filled with unhappiness, abuse, wrongful societal categorization, and alienation. She blossoms into a more complete woman in the company of Blomkvist, but in the end .... well ... in the end, there is an end, ordinary and sad as it is. The book is really all about Salander, her skill, the ultimate tragedy of her life and her disdain for all authority and societal norms. While she doesn't hate all men (particularly not Blomkvist), she does believe most males are idiots and a good percentage of us abusive and misogynist.<br /><br />Blomkvist is portrayed as the opposite of the male chauvinist pig. He does have his \\"piggish\\" qualities, but they are not founded in a dislike of women. The problem with the Salander-Blomkvist relationship is that both are incapable of and uninterested in discussing their relationship. They are together because of mutual sleuthing skills and sex. As such, it can't end well, one might think.<br /><br />Blomkvist's other love interest, Erika Berger, is a flashy successful business woman, Blomkvist's full equal and business partner. She actually is not a very interesting character. The old man, Henrik Vanger, is well portrayed and ably characterized as the elder statesman of his huge and dysfunctional family.<br /><br />I liked the book and I would recommend it to most of my friends and acquaintances. 0307454541|5.0|\\"One more man who hates women,\\" Lisbeth Salandar quotes near the end of the book. It's a fitting line, given the multiple plots that cross the novel. In fact, the original Swedish title of the novel is \\"M&auml;n som hatar kvinnor\\", which translates to \\"Men Who Hate Women\\". Without giving much of the story away, I can safely say that this  novel is, absolutely, about men who hate women. And yet at the same time, it's not about these things at all.<br /><br />The novel is, at its core, the story of a journalist (Mikael Blomkvist) and a young hacker (Lisbeth Salander), who come to work together to uncover the 40-year old mystery of a missing girl in Sweden, Harriet Vanger. Other plots weave their way in and out-- Blomkvist's humiliation as a story he writes is proven to be false and he is sentenced to prison for libel; Salander's person turmoil; the skeletons buried deep in the cupboards of the Vanger family-- and all culminate in an ending that I honestly did not see coming.<br /><br />Larrson, may he rest in peace, has skill. He took a plot that is, simply, about men hating women in horrible, awful, abusive way, and turned it into a novel that is at once horrifying and shocking and elegant and beautiful. The mystery of Harriet becomes an obsession for her uncle, Henrik, who hires Blomkvist to investigate her disappearance forty years after the fact. There are dozens of characters, locations, and subplots, and Larrson weaved them together with expertise and skill.<br /><br />The book took me a long time to read (about four days), but that's because it's a lot more dense than any of the books I've read lately. Pick the book up in the store one day, see how small the text is on the page! Whew! But wow, A+ all the way. If you have the patience to work through it (it's not boring, just difficult to absorb because it's so complex), then you should absolutely give it a try. You'll find yourself addicted.

B002GZQY6Q|3.0|Porcupine Tree has always had terrific ideas and this album isn't any different in that respect.  But generally it's what they do with all the ideas that creates so much of the real magic, and it's what turns 3-star albums into 5-star albums.  'The Incident' has about twice as many musical ideas as it should, and sadly, many of those are very much under-developed -- it's just too many things stuffed into way too small of a space.    'Occams's Razor,' which kicks off the album is a case in point.  It starts out with the repetion of a few really heavy-sounding D chords, and on the second repeat includes a little E to G# melody over the top of the chords.  So far, so good.  But then that's as far as that idea ever goes.  The rest of the song has some seemingly unrelated quiet noodling on accoustic guitar followed by a reprise of the D chords, but it all seems fairly pointless.  We don't ever hear this idea again throughout the whole album (this is not entirely true -- it is repeated later in 'Degree Zero', but almost as a verbatim repetion, rather than a differently restated or developed idea).  And, in general, this doesn't have to be a bad thing -- a couple of stand-alone chords never really hurt anybody, but in this case it's quite symptomatic of the stand-alone nature of many of the other ideas on the album.    The third track, 'Great Expectations,' starts out very promising with a simple accoustic guitar and vocal part, which comes around for a second verse with some ambient guitar and synthesizer sounds added behind it.  It's at this point where the song seems to be going somewhere, but then just as quickly it's over and we're into 'Kneel and Disconnect,' which is musically unrelated to 'Expectations.'  Why quit one idea and move onto another when you weren't done with the first one?    There are plenty of other examples of this on the album, including, for me, the most eggregious example, which is the strummed accousitic guitar part at the end of 'The Seance.'Every time I hear it I think What was that all about?  This riff comes out of nowhere, is repeated four times, and ends just as abruptly as the song segues into 'Circle of Manias.'  The only relation to anything seems to be that the riff is in 9/8 time and so is 'Circle.'  But that's not enough to make a real connection between the two songs -- it just ends up sounding like fragments of music stuck together.  Just about every song on the album that comes in at two and half minutes or less seems underdeveloped.  And even some of the longer tracks suffer somewhat from the same problem.  The five-minute title track is split up into two very clear A and B sections, neither of which seems to have anything to do with the other, other than the fact that they're both tracked into the same song on the disk.    It's not to say that all the songs are underdeveloped.  'Time Flies' has about four pretty simple ideas and yet manages to be a really satisfying 11-minute song.  The thing that really sells 'Time Flies' for me is the amount of space and breathing room that it's got.  All the ideas have a chance to settle and be heard, so it gives you something to grab on to.  It's got a simple accoustic part with a repeating rhythmic figure; repeating chords that you hear on each successive verse and chorus; some denser sections; an extended middle section of completely different music that takes it's time building up to nice noisy, tortured guitar solo -- it's all great.  The much shorter 'Circle of Manias' starts out with an incredibly excellent and evil-sounding opening riff, and then the rest of the song is pretty much just expansion on this one riff and they squeezed out as much as they could get out of it.  This song is great for many reasons, not the least of which is because there are some dynamics in there.  At one point, the guitar keeps repeating the opening riff, but quiets down with the drums a bit to give some space for the keyboards to take a prominent role for a bit and have a bit of interplay between instruments.  No fragments at all in that song -- every part has a purpose.    And so it ends up being an album of half really good stuff and half not too bad but really not great stuff.  So, for a Porcuipne Tree album, 'The Incident' is just OK.  But when I go back and listen to previous albums, there was obviously more time, craft and patience put into them.  And speaking of that, special mention must be made Gavin's drumming which is usually such a highlight, but along with many other things on this album was also a bit of letdown.  There isn't nearly as much of the hard discipline and preparation that I've heard so clearly in his playing on previous albums.  This time it just sounded like he was on auto-pilot for most of it.  It's all kind of a loose hodge-podge of Gavin-sounding ideas from his bag of tricks without any clear focus.  Much of it sounds like he didn't really meant it -- it feels like he's just playing along with a song in 4 or one in 3, and if he were to play through the song again, the fills and patterns might be a bit different.  So, it ends up just being kind of Gavin background drumming and not a real difinitive statement.  Notable exceptions are, again, 'Circle of Manias' and sections of 'Time Flies' where he seemed to have an idea of the kind of drum part he wanted to play over each section, each with some kind of recurring idea in it that he stuck to.

So versus the Complete Stone Roses import DVD I have, we're basically missing the TV appearances on disc 2 of that set, but for what you get here, it's probably more important to have the Blackpool show. Too bad - it'd have been nice to have some insights/narrative from Squire, who is arguably the best guitarist of that generation.  All in all, though, a nice packaging/remaster of one of the key rock albums of the last 20 years - if you've never owned it, this is a terrific edition to get just for the album itself and the DVD, and if you only have the original edition, this one is a near-revelation.

For example it has Hitler referring to a battle in \\"1948\\", when it is obviously 1848. Another example: \\"It would be a stake to think that the followers of various political parties...\\". Surely that should be \\"mistake\\", not \\"stake\\". Unfortunately this edition is riddled with errors such as this, which is only a reflection of lazy translation and proofing. As a result, I can't be 100% sure that it is accurate, so will have to cross-reference with another edition.

B002BSHXJA|5.0|Much has been made lately of the Beatles' mono recordings, and yet this set proves to be worth the hype.  A feast for both the eyes and ears, \\"The Beatles in Mono\\" presents listeners with miniature versions of the original Beatles LP containers (reproduced in precise detail right down to the inner sleeves), beautifully remastered sound that is not over-equalized or compressed, and a handsome outer white container that is durable and understated, perfect for display.<br /><br />Overall the Beatles' mono sound is punchier, more immediate, and much cleaner than the stereo versions. Plus you don't get the extreme channel separation as on the stereo versions of the early albums (which to be fair was quite common in 1960s stereo recordings). The Beatles reportedly were more involved with the mono mixes (at least through Magical Mystery Tour), and most purists will tell you the mono versions are the definitive Beatles recordings. (Although I personally have doubts as to how \\"involved\\" they really were on the mixing of their earlier albums, since their tour schedule only allowed brief respites for recording purposes).<br /><br />From a sound quality standpoint, there is much greater dynamic range on the earlier albums than on their previous CD incarnations (\\"Beatles for Sale\\" is particularly improved), while the mono versions of Sgt. Pepper and the White Album simply must be heard to be believed.<br /><br />There is some truth to the statement that The White Album has no definitive version (the stereo and mono mixes were largely created at the same time, and the Beatles were more involved in the stereo mixes than on previous albums, and were now using 8-track equipment). Still, the mono White Album is a beast to reckon with. Ideally, one should probably own both versions.<br /><br />Conversely, the mono version of Sgt. Pepper is superior in every way to the stereo. Tracks like \\"LSD\\", with its psychedelic phasing effects, and \\"She's Leaving Home\\", heard here at a faster speed which makes it much less morose, blow away their stereo counterparts. John Lennon, George Harrison and George Martin are all on record saying that the mono version of Pepper is superior, and one listen will confirm this. It is indeed the \\"jewel in the crown\\" of the mono set, indeed of the Beatles' entire recorded output.<br /><br />This set is overpriced to be sure, and no one seems able to predict how \\"limited\\" its production run will be, but for Beatles fans this is a collector's dream. And if the CD format is indeed on its way out, at least it's making a first-class exit.s morose, blow away their stereo counterparts.
